# Architecture Document: Envoy HTTP Connection Manager

**Repository:** envoyproxy/envoy
**Output:** Write your document to `/workspace/documentation.md`

## Task

Produce an architecture document for Envoy's **HTTP Connection Manager** (HCM) subsystem. The document must explain how the major components interact to process an HTTP request from arrival on a downstream connection through to upstream dispatch. Do not simply list APIs — explain the **design**, **data flow**, and **extension points**.

## Scope

Your document must cover these four components and how they work together:

### 1. ConnectionManagerImpl
The central network filter that owns the connection lifecycle. Explain:
- How it implements `Network::ReadFilter` to receive raw bytes
- How it creates and manages `ActiveStream` objects for each HTTP request
- Its role in codec creation (lazy codec instantiation for H1/H2 vs. H3)
- Connection-level concerns: drain decisions, overload management, watermark-based flow control

### 2. FilterManager and the HTTP Filter Chain
The per-stream filter chain execution engine. Explain:
- The decoder filter chain (request path) and encoder filter chain (response path)
- How `FilterChainFactory` creates filters for each stream
- Filter iteration: how `decodeHeaders`/`decodeData`/`decodeTrailers` propagate through decoder filters
- How filters can stop iteration, modify headers, or send local replies
- The distinction between `StreamDecoderFilter`, `StreamEncoderFilter`, and `StreamFilter` (dual)

### 3. Router Filter
The terminal decoder filter that forwards requests upstream. Explain:
- Route selection: how the router uses `RouteConfiguration` to pick a cluster
- How it obtains an HTTP connection pool from `ClusterManager`
- Upstream request lifecycle: `UpstreamRequest` creation, retry logic, timeout handling
- Shadow routing and hedged request support (if applicable)

### 4. Cluster Manager and Upstream Connectivity
The upstream connection pool and cluster management layer. Explain:
- How `ClusterManagerImpl` provides connection pools per cluster
- Load balancing: how the router obtains a host from the cluster's load balancer
- Connection pool mechanics: the relationship between logical connection pools and physical connections
- How health checking and outlier detection feed back into the load balancer

## Document Requirements

1. **Component Responsibilities** — what each component owns
2. **Data Flow** — the path of an HTTP request from downstream bytes to upstream dispatch, and the response path back
3. **Extension Points** — where users extend Envoy (HTTP filters, cluster extensions, load balancers, access loggers)
4. **Error Handling** — how errors at each stage (codec errors, filter errors, upstream failures) are handled
5. **relevant source files** — reference the actual source files in the envoyproxy/envoy repository

## Anti-Requirements

- Do NOT generate a simple API listing or header-file dump
- Do NOT fabricate class names or file paths that don't exist in the repository
- Do NOT cover Envoy components outside the HCM request path (e.g., xDS config delivery, listener management)

- **`ConnectionManagerConfig`** as the primary extension seam for codec selection, filter chain creation, route config, and local reply customization.

Overview\n\nThe HTTP Connection Manager (HCM) is Envoy's central HTTP processing subsystem. It sits at the boundary between the raw TCP/UDP transport layer and the application-level HTTP semantics that filters, routers, and upstream clusters operate on. Every HTTP request that Envoy proxies passes through the HCM, making it the single most important component for understanding Envoy's request-processing model.\n\nThe HCM is not a monolithic class. It is a collaboration of four major subsystems, each with a well-defined responsibility boundary:\n\n| Subsystem | Primary Responsibility |\n|---|---|\n| **ConnectionManagerImpl** | Owns the downstream TCP connection; creates codecs; manages per-stream `ActiveStream` objects; enforces connection-level policies (drain, idle, overload). |\n| **FilterManager** | Executes the ordered HTTP filter chain for each stream; manages filter iteration state, buffering, and local reply generation. |\n| **Router Filter** | Resolves routes; manages upstream request lifecycle including retries, hedging, and shadowing; bridges the downstream filter chain to the upstream connection pool. |\n| **ClusterManager / Upstream** | Maintains per-thread cluster state; selects hosts via load balancing; manages connection pools; enforces circuit breaking, health checking, and outlier detection. |\n\nThese subsystems are layered: `ConnectionManagerImpl` creates `FilterManager`, which runs the filter chain that terminates in the Router filter, which calls into `ClusterManager`. Data flows down through this stack on the request path and back up on the response path.\n\n---\n\n## 2.                               \u2502\n                               \u25bc\n                    Upstream TCP/TLS Connection\n```\n\n---\n\n## 3. ConnectionManagerImpl\n\n### 3.1 Position in the Network Stack\n\n`ConnectionManagerImpl` (`source/common/http/conn_manager_impl.h`) implements three interfaces simultaneously:\n\n- **`Network::ReadFilter`** \u2014 receives raw bytes from the downstream TCP connection via `onData()`.\n- **`Http::ServerConnectionCallbacks`** \u2014 receives parsed HTTP events from the codec (`newStream()`, `onGoAway()`).\n- **`Network::ConnectionCallbacks`** \u2014 receives connection-level events (`onEvent()`, `onAboveWriteBufferHighWatermark()`).\n\nThis triple inheritance is the architectural seam between the transport layer and the HTTP layer. The `Network::FilterManager` (the TCP-level filter manager, distinct from the HTTP `FilterManager`) calls `onData()` with raw bytes; `ConnectionManagerImpl` feeds those bytes to the codec, which calls back into `ConnectionManagerImpl` via `ServerConnectionCallbacks` with structured HTTP events.\n\n### 3.2 Codec Creation and Protocol Detection\n\nCodec creation is intentionally deferred until the first bytes arrive on the connection. The `onData()` method calls `createCodec()` on the first invocation:\n\n```\nonData(data, end_stream)\n  \u2502\n  \u251c\u2500 [first call] hcm_ondata_creating_codec_->shouldShedLoad()?\n  \u2502    \u2514\u2500 yes \u2192 close connection (overload protection)\n  \u2502\n  \u251c\u2500 [first call] createCodec(connection, data, *this, overload_manager_)\n  \u2502    \u251c\u2500 inspects first bytes for H2 preface (\"PRI * HTTP/2.0\\r\\n\\r\\nSM\\r\\n\\r\\n\")\n  \u2502    \u251c\u2500 uses ALPN negotiation result for H3\n  \u2502    \u2514\u2500 falls back to H1 codec\n  \u2502\n  \u2514\u2500 codec_->dispatch(data)\n       \u2514\u2500 calls newStream() for each new HTTP stream\n```\n\nThe codec is created by `ConnectionManagerConfig::createCodec()`, a pure virtual method that the HCM configuration (`HttpConnectionManagerConfig`) implements. This is the primary extension point for codec selection \u2014 the configuration layer decides which codec implementation to instantiate based on listener configuration and negotiated protocol.\n\nThe `overload_manager_` is passed directly to the codec factory so that H2 and H3 codecs can register their own load shed points (`H2ServerGoAwayOnDispatch`, `H2ServerGoAwayAndCloseOnDispatch`, `H3ServerGoAwayOnDispatch`, `H3ServerGoAwayAndCloseOnDispatch`) independently of the HCM-level shed points.\n\n### 3.3 ActiveStream Lifecycle\n\nEach HTTP stream is represented by an `ActiveStream` inner class. `ActiveStream` implements `FilterManagerCallbacks`, which is the interface through which `FilterManager` communicates back to the connection layer (to send response data, close the stream, etc.).\n\n**Creation:** `newStream()` is called by the codec when it has parsed enough of a new request to create a stream. `ConnectionManagerImpl::newStream()`:\n1. Checks the `accept_new_http_stream_` load shed point; if shedding, sends a 503 local reply immediately.\n2. Allocates an `ActiveStream` and registers it in `streams_` (a list of `ActiveStreamPtr`).\n3. Creates a `DownstreamFilterManager` for the stream, passing the `ActiveStream` as the `FilterManagerCallbacks`.\n4. Calls `config_->createFilterChain(filter_manager)` to instantiate and register all configured HTTP filters.\n5. Returns the stream's `RequestDecoder` interface to the codec.\n\n**Destruction:** Streams are not destroyed synchronously. When a stream completes (either normally or via reset), `doDeferredStreamDestroy()` posts the actual destruction to the dispatcher's deferred deletion queue. This prevents re-entrant destruction when a filter or codec callback triggers stream teardown while still on the call stack. The stream is removed from `streams_` immediately but its memory is freed only after the current event loop iteration completes.\n\n**Premature reset tracking:** `isPrematureRstStream()` detects RST frames sent by the downstream client before the response is complete. This is used for security monitoring (e.g., detecting HTTP/2 rapid reset attacks) and is exposed via stats.\n\n### 3.4 Timeout Management\n\n`ConnectionManagerImpl` manages several connection-level and stream-level timers:\n\n| Timer | Scope | Behavior |\n|---|---|---|\n| `idle_timer_` | Connection | Fires when no streams are active for `idle_timeout`. Closes the connection. |\n| `connection_duration_timer_` | Connection | Fires after `max_connection_duration`. Initiates drain. |\n| `drain_timer_` | Connection | Fires after `drain_timeout` following a drain trigger. Closes the connection. |\n| `request_timer_` | Stream | Fires if the full request (headers + body) is not received within `request_timeout`. |\n| `request_headers_timer_` | Stream | Fires if request headers are not received within `request_headers_timeout`. |\n| `stream_idle_timer_` | Stream | Fires if no data is sent or received on the stream for `stream_idle_timeout`. |\n| `max_stream_duration_timer_` | Stream | Fires after `max_stream_duration` regardless of activity. |\n\nThe stream-level timers are owned by `ActiveStream` and reset on each data event. The connection-level timers are owned by `ConnectionManagerImpl` and interact with the drain state machine.\n\n### 3.5 Drain and Connection Lifecycle\n\nThe connection drain state machine has two states: `NotDraining` and `Closing`. Drain is triggered by:\n\n- The `max_connection_duration_timer_` firing.\n- The `max_requests_per_connection` limit being reached after a stream completes.\n- An external drain signal (e.g., hot restart, listener drain).\n- The `DisableHttpKeepAlive` overload action becoming saturated.\n\nWhen drain begins, `ConnectionManagerImpl` sends an HTTP/1.1 `Connection: close` header on the next response, or an HTTP/2 `GOAWAY` frame. After the `drain_timeout` elapses with no new streams, the connection is closed. For HTTP/1.1, the connection is closed immediately after the current response completes.\n\n### 3.6 Overload and Load Shedding\n\n`ConnectionManagerImpl` integrates with Envoy's `OverloadManager` through two mechanisms:\n\n**Overload Actions** (coarse-grained, binary): These are registered at construction time and polled via `ThreadLocalOverloadState::getState()`. The HCM checks two actions:\n- `envoy.overload_actions.stop_accepting_requests` \u2014 checked in `newStream()`; if saturated, new streams are rejected with a 503.\n- `envoy.overload_actions.disable_http_keepalive` \u2014 checked when sending responses; if saturated, `Connection: close` is added to force connection teardown after each response.\n\n**Load Shed Points** (fine-grained, probabilistic): These are registered by name and queried via `LoadShedPoint::shouldShedLoad()`. The HCM uses:\n- `envoy.load_shed_points.hcm_ondata_creating_codec` \u2014 checked before codec creation; allows shedding connections before any HTTP parsing occurs.\n- `envoy.load_shed_points.http_connection_manager_decode_headers` \u2014 checked in `newStream()` before the filter chain runs; allows shedding individual streams after codec parsing but before filter processing.\n\nLoad shed points are more flexible than overload actions because they can implement probabilistic shedding (e.g., shed 30% of load when a resource monitor reaches 70% saturation) rather than binary on/off behavior.\n\n### 3.7 Request Identity and Tracing Bootstrap\n\n`ConnectionManagerImpl` is responsible for assigning a request ID to each stream. The `RequestIDExtension` interface (configured via `request_id_extension` in the HCM config) generates and propagates request IDs. The default implementation uses a UUID stored in the `x-request-id` header.\n\nThe tracing span for a stream is created in `ActiveStream` during `decodeHeaders()` processing. `ConnectionManagerImpl` calls `config_->tracingConfig()` to obtain the tracing configuration, then calls `tracer_->startSpan()` with the request headers as the `TraceContext`. The resulting `Span` is stored on the `ActiveStream` and finalized in `doDeferredStreamDestroy()` via `HttpTracerUtility::finalizeDownstreamSpan()`.\n\n### 3.8 ConnectionManagerConfig Extension Point\n\n`ConnectionManagerConfig` (`source/common/http/conn_manager_config.h`) is a pure virtual interface that decouples `ConnectionManagerImpl` from its configuration source. The production implementation is `HttpConnectionManagerConfig` in `source/extensions/filters/network/http_connection_manager/config.h`.\n\nKey methods that serve as extension points:\n\n| Method | Purpose |\n|---|---|\n| `createCodec()` | Selects and instantiates the HTTP codec. |\n| `createFilterChain()` | Instantiates the HTTP filter chain for a new stream. |\n| `createUpgradeFilterChain()` | Instantiates a specialized filter chain for protocol upgrades (WebSocket, CONNECT). |\n| `routeConfigProvider()` | Returns the `RouteConfigProvider` (static, RDS, or SRDS). |\n| `scopedRouteConfigProvider()` | Returns the scoped route config provider for SRDS. |\n| `tracingConfig()` | Returns per-HCM tracing configuration. |\n| `localReply()` | Returns the `LocalReply` instance for customizing error response bodies. |\n| `accessLogs()` | Returns the list of access log instances. |\n\n`HttpConnectionManagerConfig` implements `createFilterChain()` by calling `FilterChainUtility::createFilterChainForFactories()` with its `filter_factories_` list. This list is built at configuration time from the `http_filters` proto field, with each filter's factory callback stored as a `FilterFactoryCb`.\n\n---\n\n## 4. FilterManager and the HTTP Filter Chain\n\n### 4.1 Design Philosophy\n\nThe `FilterManager` implements Envoy's HTTP filter chain as a doubly-linked pipeline. The key design insight is that the same set of filter objects participates in both the decode (request) path and the encode (response) path, but the traversal direction is reversed:\n\n- **Decode path**: filters are visited in configuration order (first to last).\n- **Encode path**: filters are visited in reverse configuration order (last to first).\n\nThis means the filter closest to the upstream (typically the Router filter, which is always last) is the first to see the response and the last to see the request. This ordering ensures that a filter can observe both the request it modified and the response to that modified request.\n\n### 4.2 Internal Structure\n\n`FilterManager` (`source/common/http/filter_manager.h`) maintains two parallel lists:\n\n```\ndecoder_filters_:  [ActiveStreamDecoderFilter A] \u2192 [ActiveStreamDecoderFilter B] \u2192 [ActiveStreamDecoderFilter Router]\nencoder_filters_:  [ActiveStreamEncoderFilter Router] \u2192 [ActiveStreamEncoderFilter B] \u2192 [ActiveStreamEncoderFilter A]\n```\n\nEach `ActiveStreamDecoderFilter` and `ActiveStreamEncoderFilter` is a wrapper around the actual `StreamDecoderFilter` or `StreamEncoderFilter` implementation. The wrapper holds:\n- A pointer to the underlying filter (`handle_`).\n- Iteration state (`iteration_state_`: `Continue`, `StopSingleIteration`, `StopAllIteration`).\n- Buffered headers, data, and trailers that have been stopped.\n- A pointer back to the `FilterManager` for callbacks.\n\n`ActiveStreamFilterBase` is the common base for both wrapper types. It implements `StreamFilterCallbacks`, giving each filter access to the stream's `StreamInfo`, `FilterState`, route, connection, and the ability to call `continueDecoding()`/`continueEncoding()`, `sendLocalReply()`, `addDecodedData()`/`addEncodedData()`, and `recreateStream()`.\n\n`DownstreamFilterManager` is the concrete subclass of `FilterManager` used for downstream streams. It overrides `onLocalReply()` to notify all filters via their `onLocalReply(LocalReplyData&)` callback before the local reply is sent, and it implements `encode*` methods to write response data back through `FilterManagerCallbacks` (i.e., back to `ActiveStream` and then to the codec).\n\n### 4.3 Filter Iteration and Status Semantics\n\nFilter iteration is driven by a set of `decode*` and `encode*` methods on `FilterManager`. Each method iterates through the filter list starting from a given position (to support resumption after a `StopIteration`).\n\n**Header status values** (`FilterHeadersStatus`):\n\n| Status | Meaning |\n|---|---|\n| `Continue` | Pass headers to the next filter immediately. |\n| `StopIteration` | Halt iteration; do not pass headers downstream. The filter must later call `continueDecoding()`/`continueEncoding()` to resume. |\n| `ContinueAndDontEndStream` | Pass headers but suppress the `end_stream` flag (used when a filter will inject a body). |\n| `StopAllIterationAndBuffer` | Halt iteration and buffer all subsequent data/trailers in the filter manager. |\n| `StopAllIterationAndWatermark` | Like `StopAllIterationAndBuffer` but applies watermark pressure to the downstream connection when the buffer fills. |\n\n**Data status values** (`FilterDataStatus`):\n\n| Status | Meaning |\n|---|---|\n| `Continue` | Pass data to the next filter. |\n| `StopIterationAndBuffer` | Halt and buffer data in the filter manager. |\n| `StopIterationAndWatermark` | Halt and apply watermark pressure. |\n| `StopIterationNoBuffer` | Halt without buffering (the filter is consuming the data itself). |\n\n**Trailers status values** (`FilterTrailersStatus`): `Continue` or `StopIteration`, with the same semantics as headers.\n\nThe distinction between `StopAllIterationAndBuffer` and `StopIteration` is important: `StopIteration` on headers only stops header processing; subsequent data and trailers will still be dispatched to the stopped filter. `StopAllIterationAndBuffer` stops everything and buffers it, so when the filter resumes, it receives headers, buffered data, and trailers in sequence.\n\n**Resumption:** When a filter calls `continueDecoding()`, `FilterManager` resumes iteration from the filter immediately after the one that stopped. It replays any buffered data and trailers before continuing to the next filter. This allows asynchronous filters (e.g., an external authorization filter waiting for a gRPC response) to pause the entire pipeline and resume it transparently.\n\n### 4.4 Data Buffering and Watermarks\n\n`FilterManager` maintains a per-stream buffer for each direction. When a filter returns `StopIterationAndBuffer`, subsequent data chunks are appended to this buffer. The buffer size is bounded by the `per_connection_buffer_limit_bytes` configuration.\n\nWhen the buffer exceeds the high watermark, `FilterManager` calls `onDecoderFilterAboveWriteBufferHighWatermark()` on `FilterManagerCallbacks`, which propagates back to `ConnectionManagerImpl` to disable reads on the downstream connection. When the buffer drains below the low watermark, reads are re-enabled. This creates end-to-end flow control: a slow upstream or a paused filter chain causes backpressure all the way to the downstream TCP receive window.\n\n### 4.5 Local Reply Path\n\nA local reply is a response generated by Envoy itself (not forwarded from an upstream). It can be triggered by any filter via `sendLocalReply()`, or by `ConnectionManagerImpl` for protocol errors, timeouts, and overload conditions.\n\nThe local reply path in `DownstreamFilterManager::onLocalReply()`:\n1. Notifies all filters (both decoder and encoder) via `filter->handle_->onLocalReply(LocalReplyData{code, body, grpc_status, is_reset})`. Filters can modify the response code or body at this point.\n2. Calls `config_->localReply().rewrite()` to apply any configured local reply body templates (supporting custom response bodies with format strings and content-type selection).\n3. Runs the response through the encoder filter chain starting from the filter that generated the local reply (not from the beginning), so that filters earlier in the chain still see the response.\n4. Writes the response to the codec.\n\nThis design ensures that access logs, tracing, and response-modifying filters all observe local replies consistently with forwarded responses.\n\n### 4.6 FilterState: Structured Inter-Filter Communication\n\n`FilterState` (`envoy/stream_info/filter_state.h`) is a typed key-value store attached to each stream. It allows filters to share structured data without coupling to each other's headers or implementation details.\n\nEach stored object has:\n- A **name** (string key, typically namespaced, e.g., `\"envoy.filters.http.rbac.access_log_hint\"`).\n- A **state type**: `ReadOnly` (immutable after first set) or `Mutable`.\n- A **life span**: `FilterChain` (stream-scoped), `Request` (survives internal redirects), or `Connection` (shared across all streams on the connection).\n- A **stream sharing** attribute: `None`, `SharedWithUpstreamInternalRedirect`, or `SharedWithUpstreamConnection` \u2014 controls whether the object is propagated to upstream connection pools for connection selection.\n\nThe `StreamSharingMayImpactPooling` attribute is particularly important for connection selection: a filter can store an object with `SharedWithUpstreamConnection` sharing, and the connection pool will use that object's value as part of the connection hash, ensuring that requests with different filter state values are routed to different upstream connections.\n\n### 4.7 Route Cache and Per-Route Configuration\n\nEach `ActiveStream` maintains a route cache (`cached_route_`). The route is resolved lazily on the first call to `route()` (typically from the Router filter's `decodeHeaders()`), and cached for the lifetime of the stream.\n\nFilters can call `clearRouteCache()` to force re-resolution. This is used by filters that modify request headers in ways that affect routing (e.g., a header manipulation filter that changes the `:authority` or path). After clearing the cache, the next call to `route()` re-runs the route matching algorithm against the current (modified) headers.\n\nPer-route filter configuration is stored on `RouteEntryImplBase` as a `perFilterConfigs` map. Each filter can define its own per-route config proto, which is parsed at configuration time and stored by filter name. Filters retrieve their per-route config via `StreamFilterCallbacks::mostSpecificPerFilterConfig()`, which walks the route \u2192 virtual host \u2192 route config hierarchy and returns the most specific config found.\n\n### 4.8 Extension Points\n\n**Filter registration:** HTTP filters are registered via the `NamedHttpFilterConfigFactory` extension point. Each filter provides a factory that:\n1. Parses the filter's proto configuration.\n2. Returns a `FilterFactoryCb` \u2014 a `std::function<void(FilterChainFactoryCallbacks&)>` that is called once per stream to instantiate and register the filter.\n\nThe `FilterFactoryCb` is created once at configuration load time and invoked for every new stream. This separation allows expensive configuration parsing to happen once while keeping per-stream instantiation cheap.\n\n**Dynamic filter configuration (ECDS):** Filter configurations can be updated at runtime via the Extension Config Discovery Service (ECDS). The `FilterConfigProvider` abstraction wraps each filter's `FilterFactoryCb` and can swap it atomically when a new config arrives. If a filter's config has not yet been received (during warming), the `MissingConfigFilter` placeholder is used, which sets the `NoFilterConfigFound` response flag and returns a 500.\n\n**Upgrade filter chains:** WebSocket and HTTP CONNECT upgrades use a separate filter chain. `ConnectionManagerConfig::createUpgradeFilterChain()` is called with the upgrade type string and a per-route upgrade config map. This allows different upgrade types to have different filter chains, and per-route configuration to override the global upgrade filter chain.\n\n---\n\n## 5. Router Filter\n\n### 5.1 Role and Responsibilities\n\nThe Router filter (`source/common/router/router.h`) is always the terminal filter in the decoder chain. It is responsible for:\n\n1. Resolving the final route and cluster from the request headers and the route cache.\n2. Creating one or more `UpstreamRequest` objects to represent upstream connections.\n3. Managing the retry and hedging state machines.\n4. Forwarding request data to the upstream and response data back to the downstream filter chain.\n5. Reporting upstream timing, response flags, and host selection results to `StreamInfo`.\n\nThe Router filter implements both `StreamDecoderFilter` (to receive the request) and `RouterFilterInterface` (to provide callbacks to `UpstreamRequest`).\n\n### 5.2 Route Resolution and Cluster Selection\n\nIn `decodeHeaders()`, the Router filter:\n\n1. Calls `callbacks_->route()` to obtain the cached route (or trigger route resolution if not yet cached).\n2. If no route is found, sends a 404 local reply and sets the `NoRouteFound` response flag.\n3. Calls `route->routeEntry()->clusterName()` (or evaluates weighted cluster selection) to determine the target cluster.\n4. Calls `callbacks_->clusterInfo()` to obtain the `ClusterInfo` for circuit-breaking and stats.\n5. If the cluster does not exist, sends a 404 and sets `NoClusterFound`.\n6. Calls `config_.cm_.getThreadLocalCluster(cluster_name)` to get the thread-local cluster handle.\n\n**Weighted clusters:** When a route has multiple weighted clusters, the Router filter selects one by generating a random number in `[0, total_weight)` and finding the cluster whose cumulative weight range contains that number. The selected cluster name is stored in `StreamInfo` for access log use.\n\n**Header manipulation:** Before forwarding, the Router filter calls `route->routeEntry()->finalizeRequestHeaders()` to apply route-level header additions, removals, and mutations. This includes setting `x-envoy-upstream-service-time`, `x-envoy-expected-rq-timeout-ms`, and any user-configured header manipulations.\n\n### 5.3 Timeout Hierarchy\n\nThe Router filter computes a final timeout from multiple sources via `finalTimeout()`:\n\n```\nglobal_timeout = route->routeEntry()->timeout()          // route-level\nper_try_timeout = route->routeEntry()->retryPolicy().perTryTimeout()\nper_try_idle_timeout = route->routeEntry()->retryPolicy().perTryIdleTimeout()\nmax_stream_duration = cluster->maxStreamDuration()       // cluster-level\ngrpc_timeout = parsed from grpc-timeout header           // request-level\nx-envoy-upstream-rq-timeout-ms = parsed from header     // request-level override\nx-envoy-upstream-rq-per-try-timeout-ms = parsed from header\n```\n\nThe effective timeout is the minimum of the applicable values. The `response_timeout_` timer is started when the first `UpstreamRequest` is created and covers the entire retry sequence. The `per_try_timeout_` timer on each `UpstreamRequest` covers only that individual attempt.\n\n### 5.4 UpstreamRequest Lifecycle\n\nEach upstream attempt is represented by an `UpstreamRequest` object. The Router filter maintains a list of active `UpstreamRequest` objects (`upstream_requests_`); normally there is one, but hedging can create multiple simultaneous requests.\n\n**Creation flow:**\n```\nRouter::decodeHeaders()\n  \u2514\u2500 createUpstreamRequest()\n       \u2514\u2500 UpstreamRequest constructor\n            \u2514\u2500 GenericConnPoolFactory::createGenericConnPool()\n                 \u2514\u2500 HttpConnPool or TcpConnPool (based on cluster protocol)\n                      \u2514\u2500 newStream(this)  // requests a stream from the pool\n```\n\n**Pool callback flow:**\n```\nConnPool::Callbacks::onPoolReady(upstream, host, address, ssl_info, protocol)\n  \u2514\u2500 UpstreamRequest::onPoolReady()\n       \u251c\u2500 stores upstream_, upstream_host_, upstream_local_address_\n       \u251c\u2500 starts per_try_timeout_ and per_try_idle_timeout_\n       \u251c\u2500 calls upstream_->encodeHeaders(request_headers, end_stream)\n       \u2514\u2500 if request body was buffered, calls upstream_->encodeData()\n\nConnPool::Callbacks::onPoolFailure(reason, transport_failure, host)\n  \u2514\u2500 UpstreamRequest::onPoolFailure()\n       \u2514\u2500 Router::onUpstreamReset() \u2192 retry or local reply\n```\n\n**Response flow:**\n```\nupstream codec parses response headers\n  \u2514\u2500 UpstreamRequest::decodeHeaders(headers, end_stream)\n       \u2514\u2500 Router::onUpstreamHeaders(response_code, headers, upstream_request, end_stream)\n            \u251c\u2500 retry_state_->shouldRetryHeaders()? \u2192 doRetry()\n            \u2514\u2500 callbacks_->encodeHeaders(headers, end_stream)  // into downstream filter chain\n```\n\n**Deferred read disable:** When the downstream connection's write buffer fills (high watermark), `UpstreamRequest::readDisableOrDefer()` is called. For HTTP/1.1 upstream connections, this disables reads on the upstream connection directly. For multiplexed connections (H2/H3), disabling reads on one stream would affect all streams on the connection, so instead the read-disable is deferred: the upstream response data is buffered in the `UpstreamRequest` until the downstream write buffer drains.\n\n### 5.5 Retry State Machine\n\nRetry logic is encapsulated in `RetryStateImpl` (`source/common/router/retry_state_impl.h`). The retry policy is parsed from the route configuration and can be overridden per-request via `x-envoy-retry-on` and `x-envoy-retry-grpc-on` headers.\n\n**Retry conditions** are a bitmask of:\n- `5xx` \u2014 any 5xx response code.\n- `gateway-error` \u2014 502, 503, 504.\n- `reset` \u2014 upstream reset before response headers.\n- `connect-failure` \u2014 connection pool failure.\n- `retriable-4xx` \u2014 409 Conflict.\n- `retriable-headers` \u2014 response headers match configured retry header matchers.\n- `retriable-status-codes` \u2014 response code matches configured set.\n- gRPC-specific: `cancelled`, `deadline-exceeded`, `internal`, `resource-exhausted`, `unavailable`.\n\n**Retry decision flow:**\n```\nonUpstreamHeaders(response_code, headers)\n  \u2514\u2500 retry_state_->shouldRetryHeaders(headers, request_headers, callback)\n       \u251c\u2500 checks retry conditions against response code and headers\n       \u251c\u2500 checks remaining retry budget (retries_remaining_)\n       \u251c\u2500 checks retry budget from cluster (ResourceManager::retries().canCreate())\n       \u2514\u2500 returns RetryDecision: Yes / No / NoRetryRequired\n\ndoRetry(is_retry)\n  \u251c\u2500 resets the current UpstreamRequest\n  \u251c\u2500 applies retry host predicates (shouldSelectAnotherHost())\n  \u251c\u2500 applies priority load for retry (priorityLoadForRetry())\n  \u2514\u2500 creates a new UpstreamRequest\n```\n\n**Host selection for retries:** `RetryStateImpl::shouldSelectAnotherHost()` consults a list of `RetryHostPredicate` objects. The built-in `PreviousHostsRetryPredicate` tracks all hosts attempted in `upstream_hosts_` and rejects any host that has already been tried (up to `host_selection_max_attempts_`). Custom predicates can be registered as extensions.\n\n**Retry budgets:** In addition to the per-route `num_retries` limit, retries consume from a cluster-level retry budget tracked by `ResourceManager::retries()`. This prevents retry storms where many streams simultaneously retry against a degraded upstream.\n\n### 5.6 Hedging\n\nHedging (`hedge_on_per_try_timeout`) launches an additional upstream request when the per-try timeout fires, without canceling the original request. The first response to arrive wins; the other request is canceled.\n\n```\nper_try_timeout_ fires\n  \u2514\u2500 onPerTryTimeoutCommon()\n       \u251c\u2500 hedge_on_per_try_timeout_ && retries_remaining_ > 0?\n       \u2502    \u2514\u2500 doHedgeRetry()\n       \u2502         \u251c\u2500 marks current UpstreamRequest as hedged (retried_ = true)\n       \u2502         \u2514\u2500 creates new UpstreamRequest (both are now active)\n       \u2514\u2500 else \u2192 doRetry() (cancel current, start new)\n\nonUpstreamHeaders() for winning response\n  \u2514\u2500 cancels all other active UpstreamRequests\n       \u2514\u2500 upstream_request->resetStream()\n```\n\nHedged requests use the same retry budget as normal retries. The `upstream_requests_` list can contain multiple active requests during hedging; the Router filter tracks which one \"won\" and discards responses from the others.\n\n### 5.7 Request Shadowing\n\nShadow requests are fire-and-forget copies of the request sent to a shadow cluster. They are configured via `shadow_policies` on the route entry and can be either header-only (no body) or streaming (full body copy).\n\n```\ndecodeHeaders()\n  \u2514\u2500 for each active_shadow_policy in active_shadow_policies_:\n       \u2514\u2500 config_.shadowWriter().shadow(cluster, headers, timeout, options)\n            \u2514\u2500 AsyncClient::send() \u2014 independent request, response discarded\n```\n\nFor streaming shadows (`streamingShadow()`), the shadow writer creates a streaming async client request and forwards each `decodeData()` and `decodeTrailers()` call to it. Shadow responses are always discarded; errors are silently ignored. Shadow requests do not affect the primary request's retry or timeout behavior.\n\n### 5.8 GenericConnPool and GenericUpstream Abstractions\n\nThe Router filter does not directly interact with HTTP or TCP connection pools. Instead, it uses two abstract interfaces:\n\n**`GenericConnPool`** (`envoy/router/router.h`):\n- `newStream(GenericConnectionPoolCallbacks*)` \u2014 requests a new stream; calls back via `onPoolReady()` or `onPoolFailure()`.\n- `cancelAnyPendingStream()` \u2014 cancels a pending `newStream()` request.\n\n**`GenericUpstream`**:\n- `encodeHeaders(headers, end_stream)` \u2014 sends request headers upstream.\n- `encodeData(data, end_stream)` \u2014 sends request body data.\n- `encodeTrailers(trailers)` \u2014 sends request trailers.\n- `resetStream()` \u2014 resets the upstream stream.\n\nThe concrete implementations are:\n- **`HttpConnPool` / `HttpUpstream`** (`source/extensions/upstreams/http/http/`) \u2014 wraps `Http::ConnectionPool::Instance` for HTTP/1.1 and HTTP/2 upstream connections.\n- **`TcpConnPool` / `TcpUpstream`** (`source/extensions/upstreams/http/tcp/`) \u2014 wraps `Tcp::ConnectionPool::Instance` for TCP tunneling (HTTP CONNECT proxying).\n- **UDP upstream** (`source/extensions/upstreams/http/udp/`) \u2014 for UDP tunneling.\n\nThe factory for these implementations is `GenericConnPoolFactory`, a typed extension point. The `generic` factory (`source/extensions/upstreams/http/generic/`) selects between HTTP and TCP implementations based on the cluster's upstream protocol configuration and the presence of a CONNECT upgrade.\n\nThis abstraction allows the Router filter to be completely agnostic about the upstream transport protocol. Adding a new upstream transport (e.g., QUIC, Unix domain sockets) requires only a new `GenericConnPool`/`GenericUpstream` implementation and factory registration.\n\n### 5.9 Upstream Filter Chain\n\nEach `UpstreamRequest` has its own `UpstreamFilterManager`, which runs a separate HTTP filter chain on the upstream side. This upstream filter chain is configured via `http_filters` in the cluster's `upstream_http_filters` field (distinct from the downstream HCM filter chain).\n\nThe upstream filter chain allows per-cluster request/response modification without affecting the downstream filter chain. The terminal filter in the upstream chain is `UpstreamCodecFilter`, which bridges the filter chain to the `GenericUpstream` interface.\n\n`UpstreamRequestFilterManagerCallbacks` implements `FilterManagerCallbacks` for the upstream filter manager, routing encode calls to the `GenericUpstream` and decode calls back to the Router filter's `onUpstreamHeaders()`/`onUpstreamData()`/`onUpstreamTrailers()`.\n\n### 5.10 Route Configuration Hierarchy\n\nRoute matching is performed by a hierarchy of objects built from the `RouteConfiguration` proto:\n\n```\nRouteConfigProvider (RDS / static / SRDS)\n  \u2514\u2500 ConfigImpl (RouteConfiguration)\n       \u2514\u2500 VirtualHostImpl[]  (matched by :authority / Host header)\n            \u2514\u2500 RouteEntryImplBase[]  (matched by path/prefix/regex)\n                 \u251c\u2500 PrefixRouteEntryImpl\n                 \u251c\u2500 PathRouteEntryImpl\n                 \u251c\u2500 RegexRouteEntryImpl\n                 \u251c\u2500 ConnectRouteEntryImpl\n                 \u2514\u2500 PathSeparatedPrefixRouteEntryImpl\n```\n\nEach `RouteEntryImplBase` carries:\n- `clusterName` / `weightedClusters` \u2014 the upstream target.\n- `timeout`, `retryPolicy`, `hedgePolicy` \u2014 per-route traffic management.\n- `rateLimitPolicy`, `corsPolicy`, `shadowPolicies` \u2014 per-route policies.\n- `hashPolicy` \u2014 for consistent hash load balancing.\n- `upgradeMap` \u2014 per-route upgrade enable/disable overrides.\n- `perFilterConfigs` \u2014 per-route filter configuration overrides.\n- `metadata` \u2014 arbitrary key-value metadata accessible to filters and load balancers.\n- `priority` \u2014 upstream priority for load balancing.\n\n**Route Config Providers:**\n\n| Provider Type | Description |\n|---|---|\n| **Static** | Route config embedded in the bootstrap or listener config. Immutable after startup. Created by `createStaticRouteConfigProvider()`. |\n| **RDS** | Route config fetched from a management server via the Route Discovery Service xDS API. Updated atomically at runtime. Created by `createRdsRouteConfigProvider()`. Multiple HCM instances sharing the same `(route_config_name, cluster)` pair share a single RDS subscription. |\n| **SRDS** | Scoped Route Discovery Service. Routes are partitioned into scopes, each with its own `RouteConfiguration`. The scope is selected per-request based on a configurable key (e.g., a header value). Allows very large route tables to be split across multiple xDS resources. |\n\n---\n\n## 6. Cluster Manager and Upstream Connectivity\n\n### 6.1 Thread-Local Architecture\n\n`ClusterManagerImpl` (`source/common/upstream/cluster_manager_impl.h`) uses Envoy's thread-local storage (TLS) system to maintain per-worker-thread cluster state. This is the most important architectural decision in the upstream subsystem: it eliminates all locking on the hot path.\n\n```\nMain thread:\n  ClusterManagerImpl\n    \u251c\u2500 cluster_map_: map<name, ClusterData>  (authoritative cluster state)\n    \u2514\u2500 tls_: TypedSlot<ThreadLocalClusterManagerImpl>\n\nWorker thread N:\n  ThreadLocalClusterManagerImpl\n    \u251c\u2500 thread_local_clusters_: map<name, ClusterEntry>\n    \u2502    \u2514\u2500 ClusterEntry\n    \u2502         \u251c\u2500 PrioritySetImpl (host sets, per priority)\n    \u2502         \u251c\u2500 LoadBalancer* (thread-local LB instance)\n    \u2502         \u2514\u2500 ConnPoolMap (per-host, per-protocol connection pools)\n    \u2514\u2500 local_cluster_name_: optional<string>\n```\n\nWhen cluster state changes (host added/removed, health state changed, cluster added/removed), the main thread posts an update closure to each worker thread via `tls_->runOnAllThreads()`. Each worker thread applies the update to its `ThreadLocalClusterManagerImpl` independently, with no synchronization required between workers.\n\nThis design means that host set snapshots on worker threads may be momentarily stale (by one event loop iteration) relative to the main thread, but this is acceptable because the alternative \u2014 locking on every request \u2014 would be far more expensive.\n\n### 6.2 Cluster Lifecycle and xDS Integration\n\nClusters are discovered and updated via the Cluster Discovery Service (CDS) xDS API. The lifecycle is:\n\n1. **Warming:** A new cluster is created but not yet made available to the router. During warming, the cluster's EDS subscription is established and the initial host set is populated. Health checks (if configured) run against the initial hosts. The cluster becomes active only after warming completes.\n2. **Active:** The cluster is available for routing. Host sets are updated as EDS pushes arrive. Load balancers are notified of host set changes via `PrioritySet::addMemberUpdateCb()`.\n3. **Draining:** When a cluster is removed (via CDS), existing connection pools are drained. New requests are not routed to the cluster. The cluster is destroyed after all connections close.\n\nEndpoint discovery (EDS) updates the `HostSet` for each priority level. Each `HostSet` contains:\n- `hosts_` \u2014 all hosts.\n- `healthy_hosts_` \u2014 hosts with no active health flags.\n- `degraded_hosts_` \u2014 hosts marked degraded but still usable.\n- `excluded_hosts_` \u2014 hosts excluded from load balancing.\n- Locality-aware subsets for zone-aware routing.\n\n### 6.3 Load Balancing\n\nThe load balancing hierarchy is:\n\n```\nLoadBalancerBase\n  \u2514\u2500 ZoneAwareLoadBalancerBase\n       \u2514\u2500 EdfLoadBalancerBase\n            \u251c\u2500 LeastRequestLoadBalancer\n            \u251c\u2500 RoundRobinLoadBalancer\n            \u2514\u2500 WeightedLeastRequestLoadBalancer\n```\n\n**`ZoneAwareLoadBalancerBase::chooseHost()`** implements locality-aware routing:\n1. Calls `chooseHostSet()` to select a priority level and locality. If zone-aware routing is enabled and the local zone has sufficient healthy hosts, it preferentially selects the local zone's host set to minimize cross-zone traffic.\n2. Calls `chooseHostOnce()` (pure virtual) on the selected host set to pick a specific host.\n\n**`EdfLoadBalancerBase`** uses an Earliest Deadline First (EDF) scheduler for weighted host selection. Each host's weight determines how frequently it is selected: a host with weight 2 is selected twice as often as a host with weight 1. The EDF scheduler maintains a priority queue ordered by each host's \"deadline\" (next scheduled selection time), ensuring perfectly weighted distribution without per-request random number generation.\n\n**`LeastRequestLoadBalancer`** extends `EdfLoadBalancerBase` with active request counting. The effective weight of each host is `weight / (active_requests + 1)^active_request_bias`, where `active_request_bias` is a configurable exponent. This causes the load balancer to prefer hosts with fewer in-flight requests, adapting to varying request latencies.\n\n**`RingHashLoadBalancer`** and **`MaglevLoadBalancer`** implement consistent hashing. They are `ThreadAwareLoadBalancer` implementations: a single ring/table is built on the main thread and distributed to worker threads as an immutable snapshot. `chooseHost(uint64_t hash, uint32_t attempt)` maps the hash to a position on the ring and walks forward to find a healthy host, with `attempt` controlling how many positions to skip for retry-with-different-host scenarios.\n\n**`RandomLoadBalancer`** selects a random healthy host from the host set using a uniform distribution.\n\nAll load balancer implementations are registered as extensions under `source/extensions/load_balancing_policies/`. The active policy for a cluster is selected by the `lb_policy` field in the cluster proto.\n\n### 6.4 Subset Load Balancing\n\n`SubsetLoadBalancer` (`source/extensions/load_balancing_policies/subset/`) wraps any other load balancer to implement metadata-based host subsetting. It is used when a cluster has `lb_subset_config` configured.\n\n**Data structures:**\n```\nSubsetLoadBalancer\n  \u2514\u2500 SubsetSelectorMap (per selector key combination)\n       \u2514\u2500 ValueSubsetMap: absl::node_hash_map<HashedValue, LbSubsetEntryPtr>\n            \u2514\u2500 LbSubsetEntry\n                 \u251c\u2500 HostSubsetImpl (filtered HostSet for this subset)\n                 \u2514\u2500 LoadBalancer* (inner LB operating on this subset)\n```\n\n**Selection flow:**\n```\nchooseHost(LoadBalancerContext* context)\n  \u2514\u2500 chooseHostIteration(context)\n       \u251c\u2500 extracts subset metadata from context (from route metadata or request headers)\n       \u251c\u2500 looks up matching LbSubsetEntry in SubsetSelectorMap\n       \u251c\u2500 found? \u2192 inner_lb->chooseHost(context) on the subset's HostSubsetImpl\n       \u2514\u2500 not found? \u2192 chooseHostForSelectorFallbackPolicy()\n            \u251c\u2500 ANY_ENDPOINT \u2192 use default LB on all hosts\n            \u251c\u2500 DEFAULT_SUBSET \u2192 use LB on the default subset\n            \u251c\u2500 KEYS_SUBSET \u2192 use LB on a subset defined by a subset of the selector keys\n            \u2514\u2500 NO_FALLBACK \u2192 return nullptr (no host selected)\n```\n\nWhen host metadata changes (via EDS), `SubsetLoadBalancer` rebuilds the affected subset entries. Each `HostSubsetImpl` maintains its own filtered host list and notifies its inner load balancer of changes.\n\n### 6.5 Connection Pool Management\n\nConnection pools are managed per-host, per-priority, and per-protocol. The `ThreadLocalClusterManagerImpl` maintains a `ConnPoolMap` for each cluster entry, keyed by `(host, priority, protocol, upstream_options_hash)`.\n\n**Pool allocation:** `httpConnPool()` calls `httpConnPoolImpl()`, which:\n1. Computes a hash key from the host, priority, protocol, and any upstream options (e.g., ALPN overrides, socket options from `FilterState`).\n2. Looks up the pool in the `ConnPoolMap`; creates a new pool if not found.\n3. Returns the pool to the caller.\n\nThe pool creation dispatches to the appropriate implementation:\n- `Http1::ConnPoolImpl` for HTTP/1.1 (one stream per connection).\n- `Http2::ConnPoolImpl` for HTTP/2 (multiple streams per connection, up to `max_concurrent_streams`).\n- `Http3::ConnPoolImpl` for HTTP/3 (QUIC).\n\n**`ConnPoolImplBase`** (`source/common/conn_pool/conn_pool_base.h`) is the shared base for all pool implementations. It maintains four client lists:\n\n| List | Contents |\n|---|---|\n| `ready_clients_` | Clients with available stream capacity. |\n| `busy_clients_` | Clients at maximum stream capacity. |\n| `connecting_clients_` | Clients whose TCP connection is still being established. |\n| `early_data_clients_` | Clients using 0-RTT early data (TLS 1.3 / QUIC). |\n\nAnd a `pending_streams_` queue for requests waiting for a client.\n\n**`newStreamImpl()` flow:**\n```\nnewStreamImpl(callbacks, can_send_early_data)\n  \u251c\u2500 circuit breaker check: resourceManager().requests().canCreate()?\n  \u2502    \u2514\u2500 no \u2192 set UpstreamOverflow flag, return nullptr\n  \u251c\u2500 ready_clients_ non-empty?\n  \u2502    \u2514\u2500 yes \u2192 attachRequestToClient(ready_client, callbacks)\n  \u251c\u2500 shouldConnect() \u2192 create new connection?\n  \u2502    \u251c\u2500 checks resourceManager().connections().canCreate()\n  \u2502    \u251c\u2500 checks load shed point: ConnectionPoolNewConnection\n  \u2502    \u2514\u2500 yes \u2192 createNewConnection() \u2192 new ActiveClient\n  \u2514\u2500 enqueue in pending_streams_\n```\n\n**`shouldConnect()`** implements preconnect logic: it may create a new connection even when `pending_streams_` is empty, if the preconnect ratio configuration predicts that a connection will be needed soon. This reduces connection establishment latency for bursty traffic.\n\nWhen a client becomes ready (connection established, stream capacity available), it dequeues from `pending_streams_` and calls `attachRequestToClient()`.\n\n### 6.6 Circuit Breaking and Resource Management\n\nCircuit breaking is implemented via `ResourceManager` (`envoy/upstream/resource_manager.h`). Each cluster has a `ResourceManager` per priority level, tracking:\n\n| Resource | Limit | Behavior when exceeded |\n|---|---|---|\n| `connections()` | `max_connections` | New connections not created; pending requests fail. |\n| `pendingRequests()` | `max_pending_requests` | New requests rejected with `UpstreamOverflow` flag. |\n| `requests()` | `max_requests` | New requests rejected with `UpstreamOverflow` flag. |\n| `retries()` | `max_retries` | Retry not attempted; request fails with `UpstreamRetryLimitExceeded`. |\n| `connectionPools()` | `max_connection_pools` | New connection pools not created. |\n\nEach `ResourceLimit` uses an atomic counter. `canCreate()` returns true if the counter is below the limit. `inc()` and `dec()` are called when resources are acquired and released. `ResourceAutoIncDec` is an RAII wrapper that calls `inc()` on construction and `dec()` on destruction, ensuring resources are always released even if an exception occurs.\n\nCircuit breaker limits are runtime-overridable via runtime keys (e.g., `circuit_breakers.<cluster_name>.<priority>.max_connections`), allowing limits to be adjusted without restarting Envoy.\n\n### 6.7 Health Checking\n\nActive health checking is configured per-cluster and runs on the main thread. The `HealthChecker` interface (`envoy/upstream/health_checker.h`) defines:\n- `addHostCheckCompleteCb(HostStatusCb)` \u2014 registers a callback invoked when a host's health state changes.\n- `start()` \u2014 begins health checking.\n\nConcrete implementations (`HttpHealthCheckerImpl`, `TcpHealthCheckerImpl`, `GrpcHealthCheckerImpl`) send periodic health check requests to each host. Results update the host's `FAILED_ACTIVE_HC` and `DEGRADED_ACTIVE_HC` health flags.\n\nHealth check results are propagated to worker threads via the same TLS update mechanism used for EDS updates. A host that fails health checks is removed from the `healthy_hosts_` set on all worker threads, making it unavailable for new requests.\n\nThe `PENDING_ACTIVE_HC` flag is set on newly added hosts that have not yet completed their first health check. These hosts are excluded from load balancing until the first check completes (or until `no_traffic_interval` elapses, at which point they are assumed healthy).\n\n### 6.8 Outlier Detection\n\nOutlier detection passively monitors upstream response results and ejects hosts that exhibit anomalous behavior. It is implemented by `DetectorImpl` and `DetectorHostMonitorImpl` (`source/common/upstream/outlier_detection_impl.h`).\n\n**Per-host monitoring:** Each host has a `DetectorHostMonitorImpl` that tracks:\n- `consecutive_5xx_` \u2014 count of consecutive 5xx responses.\n- `consecutive_gateway_failure_` \u2014 count of consecutive gateway errors (502, 503, 504, plus connection failures).\n- `SuccessRateMonitor` \u2014 rolling window of success/failure outcomes for success rate analysis.\n- `eject_time_backoff_` \u2014 exponential backoff multiplier for ejection duration.\n\n**Result reporting:** The Router filter calls `putResult()` or `putHttpResponseCode()` on the host's monitor after each upstream response. These methods update the counters and check ejection thresholds.\n\n**Ejection:** When a threshold is crossed, `DetectorImpl::ejectHost()`:\n1. Sets the `FAILED_OUTLIER_CHECK` health flag on the host.\n2. Checks `max_ejection_percent` \u2014 if too many hosts are already ejected, the ejection is skipped to prevent cascading failures.\n3. Records the ejection time and increments `eject_time_backoff_`.\n4. Calls `runCallbacks()` to notify registered listeners (including the load balancer, which removes the host from `healthy_hosts_`).\n\n**Unejection:** `DetectorImpl` runs an `interval_timer_` that periodically calls `checkHostForUneject()` for each ejected host. A host is unejected when `base_ejection_time * eject_time_backoff_` has elapsed since ejection. After unejection, the `FAILED_OUTLIER_CHECK` flag is cleared and the host is returned to `healthy_hosts_`.\n\n**Runtime-tunable thresholds:**\n\n| Runtime Key | Default | Description |\n|---|---|---|\n| `outlier_detection.consecutive_5xx` | 5 | Consecutive 5xx responses before ejection. |\n| `outlier_detection.consecutive_gateway_failure` | 5 | Consecutive gateway failures before ejection. |\n| `outlier_detection.success_rate_minimum_hosts` | 5 | Minimum hosts required for success rate analysis. |\n| `outlier_detection.success_rate_request_volume` | 100 | Minimum requests per interval for success rate analysis. |\n| `outlier_detection.success_rate_stdev_factor` | 1900 | Standard deviation factor (\u00f71000) for success rate ejection threshold. |\n| `outlier_detection.failure_percentage_threshold` | 85 | Failure percentage threshold for ejection. |\n| `outlier_detection.max_ejection_percent` | 10 | Maximum percentage of hosts that can be ejected simultaneously. |\n| `outlier_detection.base_ejection_time` | 30s | Base ejection duration (multiplied by backoff). |\n| `outlier_detection.interval` | 10s | Interval between unejection checks. |\n\n### 6.9 Host Health Flags\n\nA host's availability is determined by a bitmask of `HealthFlag` values (`envoy/upstream/upstream.h`). A host is considered healthy only when no flags are set. The flags are:\n\n| Flag | Value | Set By |\n|---|---|---|\n| `FAILED_ACTIVE_HC` | `0x01` | Active health checker: host failed a health check. |\n| `FAILED_OUTLIER_CHECK` | `0x02` | Outlier detector: host was ejected. |\n| `FAILED_EDS_HEALTH` | `0x04` | EDS: management server marked host unhealthy. |\n| `DEGRADED_ACTIVE_HC` | `0x08` | Active health checker: host is degraded (still usable but deprioritized). |\n| `DEGRADED_EDS_HEALTH` | `0x10` | EDS: management server marked host degraded. |\n| `PENDING_DYNAMIC_REMOVAL` | `0x20` | EDS: host is being removed; drain in progress. |\n| `PENDING_ACTIVE_HC` | `0x40` | Active health checker: first check not yet complete. |\n| `EXCLUDED_VIA_IMMEDIATE_HC_FAIL` | `0x80` | Active health checker: host failed immediately on first check. |\n| `ACTIVE_HC_TIMEOUT` | `0x100` | Active health checker: health check timed out. |\n\nMultiple flags can be set simultaneously. For example, a host can be both `FAILED_ACTIVE_HC` (failed a health check) and `FAILED_OUTLIER_CHECK` (ejected by outlier detection). The host is returned to the healthy set only when all flags are cleared.\n\n---\n\n## 7. Cross-Cutting Concerns\n\n### 7.1 StreamInfo\n\n`StreamInfo` (`envoy/stream_info/stream_info.h`) is the central data structure for per-request observability. It is created with each `ActiveStream` and passed to every component that needs to record or read request metadata.\n\nKey data stored in `StreamInfo`:\n- **Timing:** `startTime()`, `requestComplete()`, upstream timing (`firstUpstreamTxByteSent`, `lastUpstreamTxByteSent`, `firstUpstreamRxByteReceived`, `lastUpstreamRxByteReceived`), downstream timing.\n- **Byte counts:** `bytesReceived()`, `bytesSent()`, `requestHeadersBytes()`, `responseHeadersBytes()`.\n- **Response:** `responseCode()`, `responseCodeDetails()`, `connectionTerminationDetails()`.\n- **Upstream:** `upstreamInfo()` \u2014 host, cluster, local address, transport failure reason, upstream stream info.\n- **Addresses:** downstream remote/local addresses.\n- **Protocol:** `protocol()` \u2014 the HTTP version used.\n- **Response flags:** `setResponseFlag()` / `hasResponseFlag()` \u2014 a set of `CoreResponseFlag` values explaining why the request was handled as it was (e.g., `NoRouteFound`, `UpstreamRequestTimeout`, `RateLimited`).\n- **Dynamic metadata:** `dynamicMetadata()` \u2014 a `ProtobufWkt::Struct` for arbitrary filter-to-filter and filter-to-access-log communication.\n- **Filter state:** `filterState()` \u2014 the typed `FilterState` store.\n- **Health check:** `healthCheck()` \u2014 whether this request is a health check request (suppresses access logging by default).\n\n`StreamInfo` is the primary input to access log formatters and is also used by the tracing system to populate span tags.\n\n### 7.2 Distributed Tracing\n\nTracing is integrated at three points in the HCM:\n\n**1. Span creation (in `ConnectionManagerImpl`):**\nWhen `decodeHeaders()` is first called on an `ActiveStream`, `ConnectionManagerImpl` calls `tracer_->startSpan(config, trace_context, stream_info, decision)`. The `TraceContext` is the request header map (implementing the `TraceContext` interface for header read/write). The `decision` is computed from the `x-envoy-force-trace` header, the request ID sampling, and the configured sampling rate.\n\n**2. Context propagation (in the Router filter):**\nBefore sending request headers upstream, the Router filter calls `span->injectContext(trace_context, upstream_context)` to inject the trace context into the outgoing request headers. The `UpstreamContext` provides the upstream host and cluster information needed by some tracing backends (e.g., for setting peer service tags). This propagates the trace across service boundaries.\n\n**3. Span finalization (in `ConnectionManagerImpl`):**\nWhen the stream is destroyed, `HttpTracerUtility::finalizeDownstreamSpan()` is called. It sets span tags from `StreamInfo` (response code, response flags, upstream host, bytes sent/received, etc.) and calls `span->finishSpan()`.\n\nThe `Tracer` and `Driver` interfaces (`envoy/tracing/tracer.h`, `envoy/tracing/trace_driver.h`) are extension points. Tracing backends (Zipkin, Jaeger, Datadog, OpenTelemetry, AWS X-Ray) implement the `Driver` interface and are registered as extensions. The `Span` interface provides `setTag()`, `setBaggage()`, `setOperation()`, `setSampled()`, `spawnChild()`, `injectContext()`, and `finishSpan()`.\n\n### 7.3 Access Logging\n\nAccess logging runs at stream destruction time (and optionally at periodic intervals for long-lived streams). `ConnectionManagerImpl` calls `log()` on each configured `AccessLog::Instance` with the `LogContext` (request/response headers) and `StreamInfo`.\n\nEach `AccessLog::Instance` has an optional `Filter` that determines whether the log entry should be written. Built-in filters include:\n- `StatusCodeFilter` \u2014 log only if response code matches.\n- `DurationFilter` \u2014 log only if request duration exceeds a threshold.\n- `NotHealthCheckFilter` \u2014 suppress health check requests.\n- `TraceableRequestFilter` \u2014 log only traced requests.\n- `RuntimeFilter` \u2014 probabilistic logging controlled by a runtime key.\n- `HeaderFilter` \u2014 log only if a specific header is present/matches.\n- `ResponseFlagFilter` \u2014 log only if specific response flags are set.\n- `GrpcStatusFilter` \u2014 log only if gRPC status matches.\n- `AndFilter` / `OrFilter` \u2014 compose multiple filters.\n\nAccess log formatters (`FormatterProvider`) extract fields from `StreamInfo` and headers to produce log lines. The formatter system supports a rich set of built-in command operators (`%REQ(header)%`, `%RESP(header)%`, `%DURATION%`, `%RESPONSE_CODE%`, `%UPSTREAM_HOST%`, etc.) and can be extended with custom operators.\n\nAccess log sinks include file-based logging (`FileAccessLog`) and gRPC streaming (`HttpGrpcAccessLog`), both registered as extensions.\n\n---\n\n## 8. End-to-End Request Data Flow\n\nThis section traces a single HTTP/1.1 request through the complete HCM stack.\n\n```\n1. TCP bytes arrive on downstream connection\n   \u2514\u2500 Network::FilterManager calls ConnectionManagerImpl::onData(data, end_stream)\n\n2. Codec creation (first call only)\n   \u2514\u2500 hcm_ondata_creating_codec_->shouldShedLoad()? \u2192 close if yes\n   \u2514\u2500 createCodec() \u2192 Http1::ServerConnectionImpl\n\n3. Codec dispatch\n   \u2514\u2500 codec_->dispatch(data)\n        \u2514\u2500 Http1::ServerConnectionImpl parses HTTP/1.1 request line and headers\n             \u2514\u2500 ConnectionManagerImpl::newStream() called by codec\n\n4. Stream creation\n   \u2514\u2500 accept_new_http_stream_->shouldShedLoad()? \u2192 503 if yes\n   \u2514\u2500 new ActiveStream created, added to streams_\n   \u2514\u2500 new DownstreamFilterManager created\n   \u2514\u2500 config_->createFilterChain(filter_manager)\n        \u2514\u2500 FilterChainUtility::createFilterChainForFactories()\n             \u2514\u2500 for each FilterFactoryProvider in filter_factories_:\n                  \u2514\u2500 factory_cb(filter_manager_callbacks)\n                       \u2514\u2500 filter_manager.addStreamDecoderFilter(new FilterA)\n                       \u2514\u2500 filter_manager.addStreamDecoderFilter(new FilterB)\n                       \u2514\u2500 filter_manager.addStreamDecoderFilter(new RouterFilter)\n\n5. Request headers dispatched to filter chain\n   \u2514\u2500 FilterManager::decodeHeaders(headers, end_stream)\n        \u2514\u2500 FilterA::decodeHeaders() \u2192 Continue\n        \u2514\u2500 FilterB::decodeHeaders() \u2192 Continue\n        \u2514\u2500 RouterFilter::decodeHeaders()\n             \u251c\u2500 callbacks_->route() \u2192 route cache lookup \u2192 VirtualHostImpl \u2192 RouteEntryImpl\n             \u251c\u2500 clusterName() \u2192 \"my-cluster\"\n             \u251c\u2500 cm_.getThreadLocalCluster(\"my-cluster\") \u2192 ThreadLocalCluster\n             \u251c\u2500 finalTimeout() \u2192 15s global, 5s per-try\n             \u251c\u2500 createUpstreamRequest()\n             \u2502    \u2514\u2500 GenericConnPoolFactory::createGenericConnPool() \u2192 HttpConnPool\n             \u2502         \u2514\u2500 HttpConnPool::newStream(upstream_request)\n             \u2502              \u2514\u2500 ConnPoolImplBase::newStreamImpl()\n             \u2502                   \u251c\u2500 resourceManager().requests().canCreate()? \u2192 yes\n             \u2502                   \u251c\u2500 ready_clients_ non-empty? \u2192 yes\n             \u2502                   \u2514\u2500 attachRequestToClient(client, upstream_request)\n             \u2502                        \u2514\u2500 UpstreamRequest::onPoolReady(upstream, host, ...)\n             \u2502                             \u251c\u2500 per_try_timeout_.enableTimer(5s)\n             \u2502                             \u2514\u2500 upstream_->encodeHeaders(request_headers, end_stream)\n             \u2514\u2500 return StopIteration (waiting for upstream response)\n\n6. Upstream response arrives\n   \u2514\u2500 Http2::ClientConnectionImpl parses response headers\n        \u2514\u2500 UpstreamRequest::decodeHeaders(response_headers, end_stream)\n             \u2514\u2500 RouterFilter::onUpstreamHeaders(200, headers, upstream_request, end_stream)\n                  \u251c\u2500 retry_state_->shouldRetryHeaders()? \u2192 No (200 OK)\n                  \u251c\u2500 per_try_timeout_.disableTimer()\n                  \u251c\u2500 upstream_request.upstreamHost()->outlierDetector().putHttpResponseCode(200)\n                  \u251c\u2500 route->routeEntry()->finalizeResponseHeaders(headers, stream_info)\n                  \u2514\u2500 callbacks_->encodeHeaders(headers, end_stream)\n                       \u2514\u2500 FilterManager::encodeHeaders(headers, end_stream)\n                            \u2514\u2500 RouterFilter::encodeHeaders() \u2192 Continue  (no-op, already sent)\n                            \u2514\u2500 FilterB::encodeHeaders() \u2192 Continue\n                            \u2514\u2500 FilterA::encodeHeaders() \u2192 Continue\n                            \u2514\u2500 FilterManagerCallbacks::encodeHeaders()\n                                 \u2514\u2500 ActiveStream::encodeHeaders()\n                                      \u2514\u2500 codec_->encodeHeaders(headers, end_stream)\n                                           \u2514\u2500 bytes written to downstream TCP connection\n\n7. Stream destruction\n   \u2514\u2500 doDeferredStreamDestroy()\n        \u251c\u2500 HttpTracerUtility::finalizeDownstreamSpan(span, request_headers, response_headers, stream_info)\n        \u2502    \u2514\u2500 span->setTag(...), span->finishSpan()\n        \u251c\u2500 for each access_log in config_->accessLogs():\n        \u2502    \u2514\u2500 access_log->log(log_context, stream_info)\n        \u251c\u2500 filter->onStreamComplete() for each filter\n        \u251c\u2500 filter->onDestroy() for each filter\n        \u2514\u2500 ActiveStream removed from streams_; memory freed after event loop iteration\n```\n\n---\n\n## 9. Extension Point Summary\n\nThe HCM subsystem is designed for extensibility at every layer. The following table summarizes the primary extension points, their interfaces, and where they plug in.\n\n| Extension Point | Interface | Registration | Purpose |\n|---|---|---|---|\n| **HTTP Filter** | `NamedHttpFilterConfigFactory` | `REGISTER_FACTORY` macro | Add custom request/response processing to the filter chain. |\n| **HTTP Codec** | `ConnectionManagerConfig::createCodec()` | HCM config implementation | Select or customize the HTTP codec. |\n| **Route Config Provider** | `RouteConfigProvider` | `RouteConfigProviderManager` | Provide route configurations (static, RDS, SRDS, custom). |\n| **Load Balancing Policy** | `TypedLoadBalancerFactory` | `REGISTER_FACTORY` macro | Implement custom host selection algorithms. |\n| **Retry Host Predicate** | `RetryHostPredicateFactory` | `REGISTER_FACTORY` macro | Customize which hosts are excluded during retries. |\n| **Retry Priority** | `RetryPriorityFactory` | `REGISTER_FACTORY` macro | Customize priority selection during retries. |\n| **Generic Conn Pool** | `GenericConnPoolFactory` | `REGISTER_FACTORY` macro | Add new upstream transport protocols. |\n| **Upstream HTTP Filter** | `NamedHttpFilterConfigFactory` | `REGISTER_FACTORY` macro | Add per-cluster upstream request/response processing. |\n| **Health Checker** | `HealthCheckerFactory` | `REGISTER_FACTORY` macro | Implement custom active health check protocols. |\n| **Outlier Detector** | `DetectorFactory` | `REGISTER_FACTORY` macro | Implement custom passive failure detection. |\n| **Tracing Driver** | `Driver` | `REGISTER_FACTORY` macro | Integrate with distributed tracing backends. |\n| **Access Log Sink** | `AccessLogInstanceFactory` | `REGISTER_FACTORY` macro | Send access logs to custom destinations. |\n| **Access Log Filter** | `ExtensionFilterFactory` | `REGISTER_FACTORY` macro | Implement custom access log filtering logic. |\n| **Access Log Formatter** | `CommandParserFactory` | `REGISTER_FACTORY` macro | Add custom format command operators. |\n| **Request ID Extension** | `RequestIDExtensionFactory` | `REGISTER_FACTORY` macro | Customize request ID generation and propagation. |\n| **Local Reply** | `LocalReply::Factory` | HCM config | Customize error response bodies and content types. |\n| **FilterState Object** | `FilterState::Object` | Runtime (filter code) | Store typed data in the per-stream state store. |\n| **Load Shed Point** | `LoadShedPoint` | `OverloadManager` | Define custom load shedding decision points. |\n| **Cluster Type** | `ClusterFactory` | `REGISTER_FACTORY` macro | Implement custom cluster discovery and management. |\n| **Transport Socket** | `TransportSocketFactory` | `REGISTER_FACTORY` macro | Customize TLS and other transport-layer behavior. |\n\nAll factory-based extension points follow the same pattern: a factory class registered at static initialization time, a proto configuration message parsed at config load time, and a runtime object created per-use (per-stream, per-cluster, per-connection, etc.). This separation of configuration parsing from runtime object creation is fundamental to Envoy's performance model.\n",
